        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer989049837" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15866956787065169447">Nearly impossible to write a review for such a masterful work as this. All I can do is write some of my thoughts while reading this. It's like a psychological character study. It's the feelings that are evoked. Everything is full of descriptions, the meaning belongs to the reader. Atwood brings me to the brink, then pulls back leaving me with a sense of uneasiness. Our lives can only be interpreted by us. Which of my own memories have been blocked, or purposely left unremembered only to surface </span>
  <span id="freeText15866956787065169447" style="display:none">Nearly impossible to write a review for such a masterful work as this. All I can do is write some of my thoughts while reading this. It's like a psychological character study. It's the feelings that are evoked. Everything is full of descriptions, the meaning belongs to the reader. Atwood brings me to the brink, then pulls back leaving me with a sense of uneasiness. Our lives can only be interpreted by us. Which of my own memories have been blocked, or purposely left unremembered only to surface in other areas without an understanding. How well do we know anyone, as we carry our own secret thoughts, what do others carry.<br /><br />See? I will be pondering this book long after it's reading. Highly recommended.</span>

<div id="review_1776251741" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1776251741" />
    <a title="Hugh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32318315-hugh"><img alt="Hugh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466451007p2/32318315.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1776251741?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hugh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hugh" href="/user/show/32318315-hugh">Hugh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/841288.Cat_s_Eye">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=booker-shortlist">booker-shortlist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=read-2018">read-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=modern-classics">modern-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1776251741" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2731693434489372441">We have just started reading another historic Booker shortlist in <b>The Mookse and the Gripes</b> group. This time the year is 1989, and although <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28921.The_Remains_of_the_Day' title='The Remains of the Day by Kazuo Ishiguro' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Remains of the Day</a> is one of my favourite Booker winners, this one must have come pretty close.<br/><br/>The narrator is Elaine, an artist who has returned from a new life in Vancouver to Toronto, the city where she spent most of her formative years, to attend and supervise a career retrospective exhibition.<br/><br/>Each of the book's sections begins with a short introduct</span>
  <span id="freeText2731693434489372441" style="display:none">We have just started reading another historic Booker shortlist in <b>The Mookse and the Gripes</b> group. This time the year is 1989, and although <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28921.The_Remains_of_the_Day" title="The Remains of the Day by Kazuo Ishiguro" rel="nofollow noopener">The Remains of the Day</a> is one of my favourite Booker winners, this one must have come pretty close.<br /><br />The narrator is Elaine, an artist who has returned from a new life in Vancouver to Toronto, the city where she spent most of her formative years, to attend and supervise a career retrospective exhibition.<br /><br />Each of the book's sections begins with a short introductory chapter set in the present and proceeds to tell another part of her life story, these start in childhood and gradually catch up.<br /><br />The dominant relationship is the one with her childhood friend Cordelia. At the start of the book Cordelia is presented as a scheming and manipulative bully. This part of the story is powerful and very believable, and culminates in an incident where Elaine almost drowns after falling through ice while trying to recover a hat Cordelia has thrown off a bridge.<br /><br />Later in childhood the balance of power in their relationship changes. Elaine becomes more successful and Cordelia more needy, and it becomes clear that Cordelia and the need to resolve their relationship still haunts Elaine as an adult.<br /><br />The best parts of this book are very strong, but perhaps it is a little too long. The sections on Elaine's development as an artist and her marriages were less interesting, but did allow Atwood to find humour in the gallery curator's attempts to find meaning in the personal pieces.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2731693434489372441" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2812570544" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2812570544" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2812570544?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780770423346" href="/book/show/1033667.Cat_s_Eye">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=2019-reads">2019-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2812570544" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1146153409584601812">I read this not too long after reading Alexander Chee's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35721123.How_to_Write_an_Autobiographical_Novel_Essays' title='How to Write an Autobiographical Novel Essays by Alexander Chee' rel='nofollow noopener'>How to Write an Autobiographical Novel: Essays</a> because this was one of his favorite novels whose form he discussed. I wanted to see how Margaret Atwood deconstructs time and memory. I wasn't disappointed. Time is deliberate and audacious in this novel. It is not alluded to by chapter headings or by a change in tense. Everything happens in the present, even though the present is sometimes past. Stylistically, it is fluid with descriptions so</span>
  <span id="freeText1146153409584601812" style="display:none">I read this not too long after reading Alexander Chee's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35721123.How_to_Write_an_Autobiographical_Novel_Essays" title="How to Write an Autobiographical Novel Essays by Alexander Chee" rel="nofollow noopener">How to Write an Autobiographical Novel: Essays</a> because this was one of his favorite novels whose form he discussed. I wanted to see how Margaret Atwood deconstructs time and memory. I wasn't disappointed. Time is deliberate and audacious in this novel. It is not alluded to by chapter headings or by a change in tense. Everything happens in the present, even though the present is sometimes past. Stylistically, it is fluid with descriptions so sensory that people and places seem close. Sentences are intentionally written with comma splice errors. The book itself moves with style and grace as it captures the life and thoughts of its main character, Elaine.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>I begin to spend time outside my body without falling over. At these times I feel blurred, as if there are two of me, one superimposed on the other, but imperfectly. There's an edge of transparency, and beside it a rim of solid flesh that's without feeling, like a scar. I can see what's happening, I can hear what's being said to me, but I don't have to pay any attention. My eyes are open but I'm not there. I'm off to the side.</b>
</i><br /><br />Elaine, a painter, has returned to her home of Toronto for a showing. Although she is celebrated, she feels alone and different. She constantly compares herself to other women artists and feels ill at ease around women. Yet through her art, fans consider her a feminist. Her paintings mostly showcase the different form and faces of women. She is not a feminist, she insists, even as she tries to dissect the thought. Elaine is removed and so her cool stoicism may not make her the most liked character. But there is also young Elaine who is reticent yet resilient, whose teenage story of bullying and abuse from friends is tragic. And this is where time plays a role. How has time carved and painted Elaine? Now a successful painter and mother, will she ever be able to form meaningful friendships or relationships? Or has her art become her means of relating to the world? <br /><br /><i>
  <b>That must be what was meant by fallen women. Fallen women were women who had fallen onto men and hurt themselves. There was some suggestion of downward motion, against one's will and not with the will of anyone else. Fallen women were not pulled-down women or pushed women, merely fallen.</b>
</i><br /><br />I purchased a used copy of this novel and it's marked with coffee stains and scented with cigarette smoke. I tried to picture the last reader, which continent would he or she have read from, and did he or she read in the morning, outside, to a cool breeze, or while stuck in a windowless room with a hot pot and an astray? The edges of my cover are worn and there is a piece the shape of a triangular, torn off one end. This made it perfect. I enjoyed interacting with form and dialogue through highlighters and pens because after reading Atwood's rendition of time, I appreciate what time has done to my little paperback.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1146153409584601812" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2211693399" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Antoinette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5328952-antoinette"><img alt="Antoinette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503842818p2/5328952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2211693399?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antoinette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antoinette" href="/user/show/5328952-antoinette">Antoinette</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780770428235" href="/book/show/1211846.Cat_s_Eye">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2211693399" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3515002436896088259">Margaret Atwood puts to rest the belief that little girls are made of “sugar and spice and everything nice.” What they are capable of is nothing short of brutal.<br/><br/>We meet Elaine- she has come back to Toronto for an exhibition of her art. She is anxious about being back, as she has always felt that if she had stayed in Toronto, she would be dead. We go through two time periods- the present and to Elaine’s childhood and young adult years in Toronto. Her being in Toronto makes her memories resurface-</span>
  <span id="freeText3515002436896088259" style="display:none">Margaret Atwood puts to rest the belief that little girls are made of “sugar and spice and everything nice.” What they are capable of is nothing short of brutal.<br /><br />We meet Elaine- she has come back to Toronto for an exhibition of her art. She is anxious about being back, as she has always felt that if she had stayed in Toronto, she would be dead. We go through two time periods- the present and to Elaine’s childhood and young adult years in Toronto. Her being in Toronto makes her memories resurface- the scars of her childhood are so deeply embedded within her.<br /><br />“ There are days when I can hardly make it out of bed. I find it an effort to speak. I measure progress in steps. The next one and the next one, as far as the bathroom. These steps are major accomplishments. I focus on taking the cap off the toothpaste, getting the brush up to my mouth. I have difficulty lifting my arm to do even that. I feel I am without worth, that nothing I can do is of any value, least of all to myself.<br />What do you have to say for yourself? Cordelia used to ask. Nothing, I would say. It was a word I came to connect with myself, as if I was nothing, as if there was nothing there at all.”<br /><br />Margaret Atwood carefully flushed out Elaine- a woman who is haunted by her childhood. Trauma doesn’t go away after the trauma- it lives on in the person and shapes their lives.<br /><br />“ The past isn’t quaint while you’re in it. Only at a safe distance, later, when you can see it as decor, not as the shape your life’s been squeezed into.”<br /><br />I finished this book a few days ago. I have been thinking about it and what I wanted to say about it. This book tore at my heart for that little girl who was victimized. It made me think about other victims, including myself. Many of us live through a trauma and move forward, but it’s true that it never totally leaves you..<br /><br />This is the fourth book I have read by Margaret Atwood. She is one of the more eclectic author’s ever. Each book is so different, other than they are all exceptionally written.<br /><br />Very impactful, thought provoking book!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3515002436896088259" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3160180836" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3160180836" />
    <a title="Ron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43881851-ron"><img alt="Ron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557265090p2/43881851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3160180836?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron" href="/user/show/43881851-ron">Ron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=friendship">friendship</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3160180836" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17628139864376869761">Eileen Risely speaks of her childhood, growing up during the time of the big war. From the present, she tells the story of her life. I've always appreciated this form of story-telling - the looking back across the years. It is a form of time-travel. Atwood used this same type of narration in her later book, <i>
  <b>The Blind Assassin</b>
</i>, which I read out of publication order (note: it does not matter, the two stories are unrelated). In this book, Eileen's retrospective begins when she is just eight yea</span>
  <span id="freeText17628139864376869761" style="display:none">Eileen Risely speaks of her childhood, growing up during the time of the big war. From the present, she tells the story of her life. I've always appreciated this form of story-telling - the looking back across the years. It is a form of time-travel. Atwood used this same type of narration in her later book, <i>
  <b>The Blind Assassin</b>
</i>, which I read out of publication order (note: it does not matter, the two stories are unrelated). In this book, Eileen's retrospective begins when she is just eight years old, that age when our self is forming. What age she is now as she travels to Toronto, and back into her past life, I could not tell. She calls herself an old woman, but age is especially subjective thing looking inward. Even with her current success as an artist, I knew her tentativeness, her own uncertainty, and that it had stemmed from the past.<br /><br />Before eight, she and her family roamed the countryside. A car their home, a tent, the forests. Homeless, I thought. But no, her father is an entomologist (much like Atwood's real-life father). They settle into an unfinished home in Toronto where Eileen's father will teach as a professor. Before this, her brother was her only friend. In her own words, other girls are a mystery to her. Yet quite and shy, she will make two friends. It is the third friend, one year later, that will change the course of her grade school years, and although Eileen could not actually say, it would be much longer than that.<br /><br />I'm always a little amazed when reading an Atwood novel. The characters deeply carved, their own words come out of the page, fully formed with thought. I didn't love every step through Eileen's life (there are many years beyond the young child who first meets Cordelia). But at the end, I am entranced with her story and the many things, good-and-bad, that make up a life. Even when Eileen doesn't find what she'd hoped for there is resolution.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17628139864376869761" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4256090403013831796">Another magnificent read for me from one of my new favorite authors Margaret Atwood. She writes in the way I love, with beautiful sentences and paragraphs, filled with metaphors and similes and wonderful descriptions of the landscape and excellent character development, I really loved Elaine our protagonist as a child, I felt protective towards her when maybe she wasn't being protected enough...The adult Elaine due to events from childhood is a damaged/flawed character who looks back on her life</span>
  <span id="freeText4256090403013831796" style="display:none">Another magnificent read for me from one of my new favorite authors Margaret Atwood. She writes in the way I love, with beautiful sentences and paragraphs, filled with metaphors and similes and wonderful descriptions of the landscape and excellent character development, I really loved Elaine our protagonist as a child, I felt protective towards her when maybe she wasn't being protected enough...The adult Elaine due to events from childhood is a damaged/flawed character who looks back on her life albeit with incomplete memories. She uses painting as a way to express herself.<br />Some people may find this a difficult book to read because of the subject matter, however Atwood handles it with sensitivity which carries you through the hard parts. <br /><br />A quote from the opening page;<br /><br />But I began to think of time as having a shape, something you could see, like a series of liquid transparencies, one laid on top of the other. You don't look back along time but down through it, like water.<br /><br />Beautiful 4 and 1/2 * rounded up to 5</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/841288.Cat_s_Eye">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44487975-dannii-elle?shelf=literary-fiction-love">literary-fiction-love</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2412966168" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6803143442183306592">Actual rating 4.5/5 stars.<br/><br/>When renowned and controversial painter, Elaine Risley, returns to her home-town of Toronto it sparks in her a confrontation of her buried past. Dark childhood secrets resurface and the Risley everyone believes they know is actually as fractured as the truth hidden inside her creations.<br/><br/>This was an interesting chronicle of one woman's life. I adored how the reader is introduced to Risley in her present, older self, before we are invited to return to her childhood and exp</span>
  <span id="freeText6803143442183306592" style="display:none">Actual rating 4.5/5 stars.<br /><br />When renowned and controversial painter, Elaine Risley, returns to her home-town of Toronto it sparks in her a confrontation of her buried past. Dark childhood secrets resurface and the Risley everyone believes they know is actually as fractured as the truth hidden inside her creations.<br /><br />This was an interesting chronicle of one woman's life. I adored how the reader is introduced to Risley in her present, older self, before we are invited to return to her childhood and explore how the woman and the child become one.<br /><br />This was a poignantly dark exploration of adolescence, with topics such as bullying, sorrow, misogyny and individual prejudice discoursed upon. I especially found myself most grief-stricken when reading of the initially seemingly harmless interactions and struggles for dominance in her first girl-friendship group, which shared a stark similarity with my own. Atwood perfectly captured what it was like, at least for me, growing up with a burgeoning awareness of myself as both feminine and an individual. The simultaneous pettiness and brutality of children is also captured here and the emotions were most raw for me, throughout the relaying of this entire period.<br /><br />From here the reader moves to college and as Risley ages so do her experiences alter and shape her. Whilst all of innovation and intrigue, none quite compared to the sorrow of youth, for me however.<br /><br />This quietly clever novel is quite simply brilliant. The accumulation of life events are laid bare for the reader to discover and the journey leads us back to the beginning: to the individual this opened with.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6803143442183306592" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_176516519" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176516519" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176516519?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=canada">canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=novel">novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176516519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17112805406939142177">Not a re-read. Instead I was walking home, today. The air was warm, muggy. Generally I try and concentrate on my feet. This, I say to myself, this is the moment that I am alive. Mind though has a tendency to go were it will. So instead I remembered this book, which does from time to time nag at me on the edges of my conscious mind, and thought the following.<br/><br/>A fine late twentieth century example of the Bildungsroman, thoroughly Wordsworthian - the child is the Father of the Man <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[wel</span></span>
  <span id="freeText17112805406939142177" style="display:none">Not a re-read. Instead I was walking home, today. The air was warm, muggy. Generally I try and concentrate on my feet. This, I say to myself, this is the moment that I am alive. Mind though has a tendency to go were it will. So instead I remembered this book, which does from time to time nag at me on the edges of my conscious mind, and thought the following.<br /><br />A fine late twentieth century example of the Bildungsroman, thoroughly Wordsworthian - the child is the Father of the Man <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[well in this case the child is the Mother of the Woman, but the general principle is the same <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, and of the particular sub-type of Bildungsroman in which the author from childhood grows up to be an artist.<br /><br />What though is different is not really the gender of the central character or even that they become a painter using egg tempura rather than a writer but the shift in the central influence upon the development of the character. In this case it is not the family, it is not the school, it is not exposure to ART in capital letters, it is the role of the "friends" and the central, haunting, shocking, believable incident that transforms the central character of the book. Look at how Atwood handles her characters artistic apprenticeship in comparison to the space given over to the childhood. All the possibilities of the child flow into that knot of relationships and nothing will have as much importance to the rest of her life.<br /><br />This means that the person undergoing Bildung is not walking a predetermined path, or one with a clear set of guidelines. There is no authority, no God, what we see is the interactions of individuals. More over the 'friendship' is not purely positive, nor entirely negative but instead a state of complicity within which the balance of power shifts as individuals come to terms, or don't, with what they have participated in. The Bildung is anthropological.<br /><br />The brother's death always seemed a bit redundant to me, and yet I wonder if in a way it is the central point. Cordelia has a tendency to exist as he says pre-mortum. All of the characters have a tendency to exist, until they don't. Their collisions have measurable impacts, even if the change down is not physically apparent. Their oscillations are an exercise in particle physics. The brother sits in a tree to watch a ball game in the USA as in passing poor citizens do in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/175675.Ragtime" title="Ragtime by E.L. Doctorow" rel="nofollow noopener">Ragtime</a>. Are there co-incidences in physics? Do random events occur in literature?<br /><br />That central childhood incident however remains shocking to me, possibly because of the style, or maybe the ordinariness and familiarity of the relationships and bullying. It creates a hardness within herself, a shield that she can use to push and shove, a toughness that is also damaging?<br /><br />Then we are back to a portrait of the artist as a young woman - isn't that one of the first lessons in story-telling, to write what you know?<br /><br />While egg tempura! What an image, a return to the past in artistic technique, a rediscovery just as the entire book is the attempt by the adult to recapture her own past.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17112805406939142177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=life-stages">life-stages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=arts">arts</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1312979668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14324321848994402008">When I was considering whether to read this or not, what flashed through my head was, “Do you really want to read a book about bullying?” I knew this was the book’s central theme. I thought, “What can I learn from this?” I knew, even before picking up the book, how despicable such behavior is. I knew where I stood, so I wondered what more could be learned.<br/><br/>By reading this book one experiences on an emotional level the cruelty and the fear and anguish bullying inflicts on another. The experience b</span>
  <span id="freeText14324321848994402008" style="display:none">When I was considering whether to read this or not, what flashed through my head was, “Do you really want to read a book about bullying?” I knew this was the book’s central theme. I thought, “What can I learn from this?” I knew, even before picking up the book, how despicable such behavior is. I knew where I stood, so I wondered what more could be learned.<br /><br />By reading this book one experiences on an emotional level the cruelty and the fear and anguish bullying inflicts on another. The experience becomes excruciating personal. It is not something you are soon to forget. A deeper understanding is achieved of the pain and grief involved and of its long-term effects. I am very glad I read the book. I have never been bullied. I now look on such behavior with a fuller understanding.<br /><br />This book is about a trio of girls who bullied a fourth. They are eight, nine years old when the bullying starts. Why this happens, the consequences of bullying and how adults react are all explored. In depth. The book follows Elaine Risley, the girl who is bullied, from her earliest years to when she is middle aged. She has become a prestigious artist and is having a retrospective of her work in Toronto, but this is also where she grew up and where the bullying occurred. Elaine thinks back, remembers and draws conclusions about what she wishes could have been different.<br /><br />The story is told in “parts”, each part split into chapters. Each part begins in the present, i.e. at the time of Elaine’s retrospective, which is probably in the 1980s. She is telling her story; we are hearing her point of view. Her thoughts in the present drift back to events in her past. Past events are told in chronological order, so each new flashback starts where the last one ended, making the story easy to follow.<br /><br />The reader sees life in the 40s, 50s, 60s and 70s. We look at how life has changed during this time-period. Elaine’s relationship with those in her family changes of course too. This is a coming-of-age story. It is about her relationship with her brother. It is about being a woman, being employed and being independent. The book is not just about bullying! It is about art and art criticism. The book keeps you thinking, on not just one topic but many. <br /><br />The book has wonderful lines. Look at these:<br /><br />“Knowing too much about other people puts you in their power, they have a claim on you, you are forced to understand their reasons for doing things and then you are weakened.”<br /><br />“Another belief of mine: that everyone else my age is an adult, whereas I am merely in disguise.”<br /><br />“There is never only one of anyone.”<br /><br />“She’s never learned the intricacies of male silences.”<br /><br />“Love blurs your vision; but after it recedes, you can see more clearly than ever. It's like the tide going out, revealing whatever's been thrown away and sunk: broken bottles, old gloves, rusting pop cans, nibbled fish bodies, bones. This is the kind of thing you see if you sit in the darkness with open eyes, not knowing the future. The ruin you've made.” <br /><br />“What we share may be a lot like a traffic accident but we get one another. We are survivors of each other. We have been shark to one another, but also lifeboat. That counts for something.”<br /><br />“We make love for the comfort of it.”<br /><br />“He is like an apple after a prolonged gluttonous binge.”<br /><br />“Little girls are cute and small only to adults. To one another they are not cute. They are life sized.” <br /><br />“You don't look back along time but down through it, like water. Sometimes this comes to the surface, sometimes that, sometimes nothing. Nothing goes away.” <br /><br />“Art is a hijacking of the visual.”<br /><br />“Art is something you can do at home, in your spare time.”<br /><br />“You can draw art very well, but you cannot draw life.”<br /><br />“Today on the way home, it snows. Big, soft caressing flakes fall onto our skin like cold moths; the air fills with feathers.” <br /><br />“He looks like someone who will later turn up on a stamp.”<br /><br />The book is described as being disturbing, hilarious and compassionate. It is all three. The lines upset, make you laugh and cry and leave you thinking.<br /> <br />The audiobook is very, very well narrated by Laurel Lefkow. She makes Elaine sound real, both when she is young and as she becomes older. Her thoughts and her emotions feel genuine. It felt like Elaine was talking to <u>
  <b>me</b>
</u>. <br /><br />This is definitely my favorite book by the author. It has excellent writing, which I also saw in her other novels of a more dystopian bent, but the realism of this novel made it much easier for me to relate to the characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14324321848994402008" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

When I was 16 and read it for the first time, that was as close to a transcendental experience as I've ever had. Since then, I have re-read it roughly twenty thousand times, always whenever I need to just submerge myself in drenching beauty and angst.</span>

<div id="review_209415700" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/209415700" />
    <a title="Mariel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/647547-mariel"><img alt="Mariel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451321467p2/647547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/209415700?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mariel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mariel" href="/user/show/647547-mariel">Mariel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">barbarism begins at home</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mariel by: </span>
      doll house
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer209415700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3871153014989132785">Cordelia: Hey! You think I'm never lonely because I'm so cute and popular? I can be surrounded by people and be completely alone. It's not like any of them really know me. I don't even know if they like me half the time. People just want to be in a popular zone. Sometimes when I talk, everyone's so busy agreeing with me, they don't hear a word I say.<br/>Buffy: Well, if you feel so alone, then why do you work so hard at being popular?<br/>Cordelia: Well, it beats being alone all by yourself.<br/>(from Buffy th</span>
  <span id="freeText3871153014989132785" style="display:none">Cordelia: Hey! You think I'm never lonely because I'm so cute and popular? I can be surrounded by people and be completely alone. It's not like any of them really know me. I don't even know if they like me half the time. People just want to be in a popular zone. Sometimes when I talk, everyone's so busy agreeing with me, they don't hear a word I say.<br />Buffy: Well, if you feel so alone, then why do you work so hard at being popular?<br />Cordelia: Well, it beats being alone all by yourself.<br />(from Buffy the Vampire Slayer season one episode Out of Sight, Out of Mind.)<br /><br />Cordelia, I love you (most of the time), but it reaaaaally doesn't beat being alone all by yourself. <br /><br /> I've been through the ringer, the rinse (and repeat) cycle, what have you, time and again. Bullied? Been there and done that and got the t-shirt (a few sizes too large so the catty girls can imply I have a weight problem). I've got stories that are dead on the spot to kill all the cells for more hair and nail curlingly embarrassing cringe worthy trips down memory lane. My saving grace was that the bullies were not my <i>friends</i>. I should say that I knew that they were not my friends. Elaine's so-calleds are the Ike Turner variety. We meant it, baby. You had it coming, baby. Or more like a slot machine that lets you win once in a great while to keep you thinking that you might just win this time. It's the jackpot of approval. If you do win they'll just take it back like the Florida lotto taking back the money they said they gave to education. (The dreaded backhanded compliment! Pleaaaaaaaaaaaaase, anything but that. Just watch the French film Ridicule and you will be a pro.)<br /><br />Not too long ago I heard a great bit of wisdom on coughs teen show Pretty Little Liars coughs (Don't judge me! It's addicting) from most likely to be suspected of murder town scapegoat, Toby, to the lesbian in disguise, Emily, that if you change yourself to be what others want you to be they still won't like you. What they really want is for you to go away. Okay, that wasn't really anything I hadn't figured out on my own but I thought it was pretty apt all the same. Mission: Impossible. When I was a miserable kid I'd go through those vicious encounters and take apart everything I might have done differently to avoid it. I always came up with not a damned thing. I could have skipped school, again. Unlike Elaine, my tormentors were mostly boys. (Something about getting breasts at age ten. It took a very long time to get over that.) I did find it awfully irritating all of her assessments about getting along with boys, boys being so much easier, blah blah blah. I'll get to the sexist undertones later. It annoyed the fuck out of me and greatly contributed to my three star rating. (Okay, I'll do it now. I'll go on many different tangents and might not remember later. It read like I was being lied to, either on purpose or in a sidestepping all of the ramifications of bullies and those who get bullied by making it all about the bully. One, it's crap. There were shit men in her life. Why was it okay for them to call girls dogs and bitches? Because she "knew" they didn't mean HER? I could have used some feel for how the hell she took these sides beyond stereotypes about tomboys and a couple of bitchy girls. It just felt generic to me, I guess. Don't go giving me product of your times, Elaine! I'm not buying the claimed ease. There's got to be more to this story.)<br /><br />My brother told me the only thing that ever helped: people are only the cool kids when they are around other people just like them and they get to have some unspoken (or maybe it is spoken) rule that this is what is cool. Cat's Eye in the start of the book is a more intriguing read than in the end for having that sense that it could have gone another way. Was it really the end all of Elaine's life? It isn't anything you do yourself that brings it on. It is who you are with that determines the drift. If Cordelia had brought that used sanitary napkin to a different set of girls they might have crucified her for it. Not to mention a number of things done by Carol or Grace. These are not sophisticated girls. How DID it happen in the first place, other than the pain in the ass need to be liked? Elaine did not understand it, merely prayed for the winds from the star I see tonight to blow the candle out and make that wish for it to be Carol's turn come true. Cat's Eye is a middle aged lady waiting for someone else to explain it to her. In their sinister playtime they are bended to Cordelia's whims. It was her golden age because of them. The three girls picked up her mean brain waves and her itch or whatever it was was scratched (no doubt it has to go somewhere). (I hope she didn't have kids for an all new audience.) Elaine doesn't know what forces called Cordelia to her torment. No latter day "My, this neighborhood has changed!" walk through could have been as good as feeling that anything might happen. Cat's Eye was missing that feeling too often and it was completely gone in the later half. Damn Elaine was too busy wishing it had never happened to give me the insight that I wanted! The painful self consciousness (if not awareness) that was so real in the memories of childhood disappears into bland narcissism.<br /><br /> I mean, they tried to murder her! Telling Elaine's mother that she had some school thing was a deliberate attempt to prevent Elaine's body from being found. That's sick as shit! There's no way it was all about power. They had to have been gleeful about it as they pushed Carol to be the one to tell Elaine's mother. My court of law says they are fully culpable. Nuh uh, no way did Elaine "switch places" with Cordelia. If Cordelia was acting out abuse from her father and two sisters, what were Carol and Grace doing? Escalating torture? Little sociopaths of Canada? Not that I'm sure that Elaine realized the implications of their Elaines whereabouts story to Mother so she wouldn't go looking for her... Exactly! She STILL doesn't get it. Why?! Would she have done any differently if it were Carol or Grace in her watery hole? Or Cordelia? I think not. Is this why it does not have meaning to her? Because it is what you do? <br /><br />I was expecting it be revealed that Elaine was one of those unreliable narrators. An unreliable narrator in the sense that she did not know herself or her own story to tell it. I wearied of this. Was Elaine's part time sexism really because of what Cordelia and her cronies did to her? Sure, she claims to have such an easy time of it with men... Um, what about that creepy as shit art professor who you let treat you like his bitch? Or going into the nuthouse 'cause your relationship didn't work out? Or going back for memory sex with him when you've got your comforting I can take or leave you second husband? How did Elaine really feel about anyone? I have a bad feeling that she could easily have been Carol and Grace herself (she did admit to enjoying the time or two when it was Carol's turn) for all her lack of independent thought from generalizations and rationalizations. I'm thinking that's the real reason she is a woman grown and not over it.<br /><br />What I really want to say is there are long, long parts of life stuff that reads like when someone tells you they lived some "You know how it goes" life thing, and either doesn't want to admit they aren't like what they think everyone else is supposed to be like, or they have no personality at all. I can't decide which is which but I didn't care after a while. I don't know if her experience as a little girl had everything to do with Cordelia if she didn't remember it until much later in life. There HAD to be something more to it than that. What Cat's Eye really read like to me was trying to get around saying what one really feels because they are afraid it is the wrong thing to say, or will make them look bad. The ending felt to me like Elaine is going to be uneasy until her dying day if she never gets that final reassurance from Cordelia. It's okay, I like you. (Isn't that why people like bullies? They feel cool if that person who doesn't like other people likes <i>them</i>.) What I do know is that shit about girls being more cruel than boys is bull shit. Man, that really bothered me about this book. Watch the film If... (Malcolm McDowell landed his A Clockwork Orange role due to this performance) or read Robert Graves's memoir Goodbye to All That. Buggering little buggers, man! <br /><br />It's a hard thing to do, anyway. I didn't believe that Winona Ryder's character wanted Heather #1 to like her. She was way too collected for that. The other two Heathers, yeah. Mean Girls she wanted to be her. Did Elaine want to be Cordelia? Maybe this speaks badly of me... (I can have a mean tongue myself. It has chased away attempted bullies in my adult life. Don't knock it!) I didn't think it was that bad! It's not like she tried to kill her! I was more bothered by the creepy I'm upset I'm not as young and hot as I want to be whingeing. There had to have been more to it than I met this girl and she didn't like me. Maybe Elaine wants to "win" over and over again and will never grow up. I can guess what that winning entails and I'm glad to not be reading Cat's Eye any longer. If there wasn't any more to it... Why did I ever waste my time worrying or getting upset when I'm not liked? <br /><br />John Malkovich once said that how someone feels about something he says is about them, not him. I aspire to one day be like John Malkovich. In reality I'm more like Morrissey. How dare you don't like me! Sobs. Cutting remark. Sobs. I'm gonna die aloooooone. Sobs. (Sobs. Sobs. Sobs! Poor Morrissey!) I'm thinking John Malkovich has got something here, other than the whole attempted murder part, of course. That would be hard to brush off. Unless you were Elaine and seemingly incapable of original thought.. If one could grow thick skin... <br /><br /><br />Cordelia: Harmony, shut up. Do you know what you are, Harmony? You're a sheep.<br />Harmony: I'm not a sheep.<br />Cordelia: You're a sheep. All you ever do is what everyone else does just so you can say you did it first. And here I am, scrambling for your approval, when I'm way cooler than you are 'cause I'm not a sheep. I do what I wanna do, and I wear what I wanna wear. And you know what? I'll date whoever the hell I wanna date. No matter how lame he is. <br />(Buffy again! From Bewitched, Bothered and Bewildered. That's season two for the lame lay person.)<br /><br />Cordelia is a sheep. Elaine is a sheep. Carol is a sheep and Grace is a sheep. If you are as cool as who you are with... Not wolves in sheeps clothing but sheep in sheeps clothing acting as their own shepherds in some closed off pasture. And they are also the mongrels (I mean dogs). Lassie IS a bitch. Baaaaaah. That's the moral of this story as I see it. It must suck to be a sheep and it must doubly suck to be caught in with other sheep. It sucked for me to be caught with this sheep (Elaine).</span>
  <a data-text-id="3871153014989132785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1336378422" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1336378422" />
    <a title="Maxwell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell"><img alt="Maxwell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539318702p2/1381728.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1336378422?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maxwell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maxwell" href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell">Maxwell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385260077" href="/book/show/220520.Cat_s_Eye">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1381728-maxwell?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1336378422" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12742337193684100709">I've yet to be disappointed by any of Margaret Atwood's stories. She has such a keen sense of the human experience, a strong skill of observation, and she comments so wonderfully on these insights that I am moved and troubled in reading her works. She forces me to think about my individual experience as well as its reflection in light of our connected humanity.<br/><br/><i>Cat's Eye</i> follows Elaine Risley, an elderly woman, famous for her controversial paintings, as she prepares for a retrospective show of he</span>
  <span id="freeText12742337193684100709" style="display:none">I've yet to be disappointed by any of Margaret Atwood's stories. She has such a keen sense of the human experience, a strong skill of observation, and she comments so wonderfully on these insights that I am moved and troubled in reading her works. She forces me to think about my individual experience as well as its reflection in light of our connected humanity.<br /><br /><i>Cat's Eye</i> follows Elaine Risley, an elderly woman, famous for her controversial paintings, as she prepares for a retrospective show of her work and reflects on her childhood. Particularly, Elaine is haunted by the memories of Cordelia, her best friend and biggest bully from her school days. That complicated relationship affects how Elaine handles her marriage(s), family life, and career. <br /><br />Like I said, Atwood is so observant. Her attention to detail and almost hyper-awareness of the human psyche is so impressive. The story is excellently crafted as well. Details resurface time and again to link the narrative into a cohesive piece, much like the cat's eye is a motif in Elaine's paintings and life story. <br /><br />I didn't expect much going into it, actually knowing very little about the story, but I was pleasantly surprised. It makes me excited to continue to read from Atwood's oeuvre. <b>4.5/5 stars</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="12742337193684100709" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_305988029" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/305988029" />
    <a title="Maciek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek"><img alt="Maciek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280839159p2/2969647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/305988029?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maciek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maciek" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek">Maciek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/6897493-cat-s-eye">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=canadian-literature">canadian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=booker-long-and-shortlist">booker-long-and-shortlist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=own-in-paperback">own-in-paperback</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=owned-books">owned-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer305988029" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7496382043429947158">So what do you do when you're a girl and you have this peculiar friend, who is also your worst abuser, but for some reason you hang out with her, go to the same school and all that jazz? Why, you become a controversial painter, get involved with some creepy men, and then sort of go on from there. Good thing your brother is a semi-genius fascinated with spacetime and all its promises, and your dad is this entomologist who travels all over the country with his family. We don't want things to be to</span>
  <span id="freeText7496382043429947158" style="display:none">So what do you do when you're a girl and you have this peculiar friend, who is also your worst abuser, but for some reason you hang out with her, go to the same school and all that jazz? Why, you become a controversial painter, get involved with some creepy men, and then sort of go on from there. Good thing your brother is a semi-genius fascinated with spacetime and all its promises, and your dad is this entomologist who travels all over the country with his family. We don't want things to be too normal, because that would be so boring!<br /><br /><i>Cat's Eye</i> is the story of Elaine Risley, the character in question who reflects on her life as she arrives in Toronto for a retrospective of her art. She remembers her brother, Stephen; travels over the country with her father and the life she has led. But most of all she remembers Cordelia, Cordelia who was at once her best friend and tormentor. The narrative switches between the past and the present, as Elaine tries to understand what has happened to her so far and what she should make of it. The exact same approach was used in <i>The Blind Assasin</i>, Atwood's Booker winning novel, and I dare to say a better work. There the narrative flow was smoother, transitions less jarring; it felt natural. Not so with <i>Cat's Eye</i>, which was also nominated for the Booker, but didn't win.<br /><br />The main theme of the novel is the development of relationships between girls, and the unique topic of female bullying. This isn't a theme explored often in fiction, and the theme of past experience influencing present experiences and their lasting effect becomes the main crux of the narrative. As the novel takes place in mid 20-th century Canada it is also rich in commentary about the political and social situation in the country: the relationship of the country with the British Empire during the second World War, and the forming of Canadian identity, the visit of the British royalty; another war, and the draft dodgers who arrive in Canada to avoid going to Vietnam, and the reality they faced that didn't quite met their expectations, the city of Toronto and its evolution through the years. These themes appear one after another and never feel properly developed, making their inclusiom seem to be only tactical, as if the author checking points to cover for the reading group's guide to include at the end of the text. Instead of being fully developed they have been reduced to mere remarks, whispers and utterances.<br /><br />Margaret Atwood is a fabulous writer, and reading her describe everyday situation will put you as if you were there with her, on the streets of the city or deep in the wilderness, both natural and that of the human mind. However, in <i>Cat's Eye</i> I felt that her powers weren't quite on their full display, as with her later novels <i>The Blind Assasin</i> and . Any Atwood novel is worth the time spent on reading it, and this is no exception; however, I did wish at moments that the time I spent on reading <i>Cat's Eye</i> would flow faster. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7496382043429947158" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('daIcEF/zaTUqRLfZQATSrqkkw0wNywKYB/BIeogUDqIjLkoIQqATZ6wgaP5HIbd2Q1VWeu1L+FwV9wthznlukA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('rqxrP3v4TInfwMn3dVelslOvky7MSDgX0/vJIapxMl74ID0nZqs221mkFtBycsBqud4GGCzIwtPB/Io67BxSbA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Doch am Grunde des Kraters steht bereits ein Zelt, und in diesem Zelt hockt der Tscharli, ein Ur-Bayer – respektlos, ohne Benimm und mit unerträglichen Ansichten.\n\nIn der Nacht bricht ein Schneesturm herein und schweißt die beiden wider Willen zusammen. Es beginnt eine gemeinsame Reise, unglaublich rasant und authentisch erzählt, wie das nur Politycki kann, gespickt mit absurden und aberwitzigen Abenteuern. Doch der Tod fährt in Afrika immer mit, und nur einer der beiden wird die Heimreise antreten.\n\nDieser grandiose Roman über zwei sehr gegensätzliche Weggefährten, jeder auf seine Weise von der Liebe       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3472.Margaret_Atwood">About Margaret Atwood</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3472.Margaret_Atwood"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1282859073p3/3472.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/3472.Margaret_Atwood">Margaret Atwood
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3034998817290229455">Margaret Atwood was born in 1939 in Ottawa and grew up in northern Ontario, Quebec, and Toronto. She received her undergraduate degree from Victoria College at the University of Toronto and her master's degree from Radcliffe College.<br/><br/>Throughout her writing career, Margaret Atwood has received numerous awards and honourary degrees. She is the author of more than thirty-five volumes of poetry, childr</span>
  <span id="freeText3034998817290229455" style="display:none">Margaret Atwood was born in 1939 in Ottawa and grew up in northern Ontario, Quebec, and Toronto. She received her undergraduate degree from Victoria College at the University of Toronto and her master's degree from Radcliffe College.<br /><br />Throughout her writing career, Margaret Atwood has received numerous awards and honourary degrees. She is the author of more than thirty-five volumes of poetry, children’s literature, fiction, and non-fiction and is perhaps best known for her novels, which include The Edible Woman (1970), The Handmaid's Tale (1983), The Robber Bride (1994), Alias Grace (1996), and The Blind Assassin, which won the prestigious Booker Prize in 2000. Atwood's dystopic novel, Oryx and Crake, was published in 2003. The Tent (mini-fictions) and Moral Disorder (short stories) both appeared in 2006. Her most recent volume of poetry, The Door, was published in 2007. Her non-fiction book, Payback: Debt and the Shadow Side of Wealth ­ in the Massey series, appeared in 2008, and her most recent novel, The Year of the Flood, in the autumn of 2009. Ms. Atwood's work has been published in more than forty languages, including Farsi, Japanese, Turkish, Finnish, Korean, Icelandic and Estonian. In 2004 she co-invented the Long Pen TM.<br /><br />Margaret Atwood currently lives in Toronto with writer Graeme Gibson. <br /><br />Associations: Margaret Atwood was President of the Writers' Union of Canada from May 1981 to May 1982, and was President of International P.E.N., Canadian Centre (English Speaking) from 1984-1986. She and Graeme Gibson are the Joint Honourary Presidents of the Rare Bird Society within BirdLife International. Ms. Atwood is also a current Vice-President of PEN International.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3034998817290229455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
